<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome of Tripterygium wilfordii and identification of cytochrome P450 involved in triptolide biosynthesis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-02-20"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Triptolide is a trace natural product of Tripterygium wilfordii. It has antitumor activities, particularly against pancreatic cancer cells. Identification of genes and elucidation of the biosynthetic pathway leading to triptolide are the prerequisite for heterologous bioproduction. Here, we report a reference-grade genome of T. wilfordii with a contig N50 of 4.36&#8201;Mb. We show that copy numbers of triptolide biosynthetic pathway genes are impacted by a recent whole-genome triplication event. We further integrate genomic, transcriptomic, and metabolomic data to map a gene-to-metabolite network. This leads to the identification of a cytochrome P450 (CYP728B70) that can catalyze oxidation of a methyl to the acid moiety of dehydroabietic acid in triptolide biosynthesis. We think the genomic resource and the candidate genes reported here set the foundation to fully reveal triptolide biosynthetic pathway and consequently the heterologous bioproduction. Tripterygium wilfordii is a medical plant that can produce antitumor activity compound triptolide. Here, the authors assemble its genome and identify a cytochrome P450 that can catalyze oxidation of a methyl to the acid moiety of dehydroabietic acid in triptolide biosynthesis by integrating multi-omics data."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Triptolide is a trace natural product of <i>Tripterygium wilfordii</i>. It has antitumor activities, particularly against pancreatic cancer cells. Identification of genes and elucidation of the biosynthetic pathway leading to triptolide are the prerequisite for heterologous bioproduction. Here, we report a reference-grade genome of <i>T. wilfordii</i> with a contig N50 of 4.36 Mb. We show that copy numbers of triptolide biosynthetic pathway genes are impacted by a recent whole-genome triplication event. We further integrate genomic, transcriptomic, and metabolomic data to map a gene-to-metabolite network. This leads to the identification of a cytochrome P450 (CYP728B70) that can catalyze oxidation of a methyl to the acid moiety of dehydroabietic acid in triptolide biosynthesis. We think the genomic resource and the candidate genes reported here set the foundation to fully reveal triptolide biosynthetic pathway and consequently the heterologous bioproduction.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Tripterygium wilfordii</i>, a perennial twining shrub of the Celastrales, has been used medicinally for centuries, mainly to treat rheumatoid arthritis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Liu, Y. et al. Extracts of Tripterygium wilfordii Hook F in the treatment of rheumatoid arthritis: a systemic review and meta-analysis of randomised controlled trials. Evid. Based Complement Altern. Med. 2013, 410793 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d9093e800">1</a></sup>. It has been known to be a rich source of specialized metabolites. Two of these (triptolide and celastrol) are among five natural products highlighted for their great potential to be developed into pharmaceuticals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Corson, T. W. &amp; Crews, C. M. Molecular understanding and modern application of traditional medicines: triumphs and trials. Cell 130, 769–774 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d9093e804">2</a></sup>. Indeed, triptolide has been demonstrated to possess important therapeutic potential with anti-inflammatory, immunosuppressive, and antitumor activities, as well as to exhibit potentially medically relevant activity for central nervous system diseases (e.g. Parkinson’s and Alzheimer’s diseases)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Corson, T. W. &amp; Crews, C. M. Molecular understanding and modern application of traditional medicines: triumphs and trials. Cell 130, 769–774 (2007)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d9093e808">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, Y. W. et al. Triptolide exerts anti-tumor effect on oral cancer and KB cells in vitro and in vivo. Oral. Oncol. 45, 562–568 (2009)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d9093e808_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Titov, D. V. et al. XPB, a subunit of TFIIH, is a target of the natural product triptolide. Nat. Chem. Biol. 7, 182–188 (2011)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d9093e808_2">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Manzo, S. G. et al. Natural product triptolide mediates cancer cell death by triggering CDK7-dependent degradation of RNA polymerase II. Cancer Res. 72, 5363–5373 (2012)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d9093e808_3">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chugh, R. et al. A preclinical evaluation of Minnelide as a therapeutic agent against pancreatic cancer. Sci. Transl. Med. 4, 156ra139 (2012)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d9093e808_4">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zheng, Y., Zhang, W. J. &amp; Wang, X. M. Triptolide with potential medicinal value for diseases of the central nervous system. CNS Neurosci. Ther. 19, 76–82 (2013)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d9093e808_5">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Mujumdar, N. et al. Triptolide induces cell death in pancreatic cancer cells by apoptotic and autophagic pathways. Gastroenterology 139, 598–608 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d9093e811">8</a></sup>. Moreover, several derivatives of triptolide have undergone clinical trials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, L., Xu, Y., Fu, L., Li, Y. &amp; Lou, L. (5R)-5-hydroxytriptolide (LLDT-8), a novel immunosuppressant in clinical trials, exhibits potent antitumor activity via transcription inhibition. Cancer Lett. 324, 75–82 (2012)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d9093e815">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rivard, C. et al. Inhibition of epithelial ovarian cancer by Minnelide, a water-soluble pro-drug. Gynecol. Oncol. 135, 318–324 (2014)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d9093e815_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Arora, N. et al. Tu1964 Minnelide: a novel therapeutic agent for gastric adenocarcinoma. Gastroenterology 148, S–947 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d9093e818">11</a></sup>.</p><p>Currently, triptolide only can be extracted from <i>T. wilfordii</i> with extremely low yields, ranging from 0.0001% to 0.002% of dry weight biomass<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zeng, F. et al. Simultaneous quantification of 18 bioactive constituents in Tripterygium wilfordii using liquid chromatography-electrospray ionization-mass spectrometry. Planta Med. 79, 797–805 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d9093e828">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zhou, Z. L., Yang, Y. X., Ding, J., Li, Y. C. &amp; Miao, Z. H. Triptolide: structural modifications, structure-activity relationships, bioactivities, clinical development and mechanisms. Nat. Prod. Rep. 29, 457–475 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d9093e831">13</a></sup>, and the plant cannot be cultivated at a large scale as contamination with its pollen renders honey poisonous, which has frequently causes poisoning events in areas where this medicinal plant is cultivated. Although significant efforts have been devoted to improving chemical synthesis, current routes are limited to yields of less than 1.64% due to the structural and stereochemical complexity of triptolide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Buckanin, R. S., Chen, S. J., Frieze, D. M., Sher, F. T. &amp; Berchtold, G. A. Total synthesis of triptolide and triptonide. J. Am. Chem. Soc. 102, 1200–1201 (1980)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d9093e835">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lai, C. K. et al. Total synthesis of racemic triptolide and triptonide. J. Org. Chem. 47, 2364–2369 (1982)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d9093e835_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Van Tamelen, E. E., Demers, J. P., Taylor, E. G. &amp; Koller, K. Total synthesis of l-triptonide and l-triptolide. J. Am. Chem. Soc. 102, 5424–5425 (1980)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d9093e835_2">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Goncalves, S., Hellier, P., Nicolas, M., Wagner, A. &amp; Baati, R. Diastereoselective formal total synthesis of (+/−)-triptolide via a novel cationic cyclization of 2-alkenyl-1,3-dithiolane. Chem. Commun. (Camb.) 46, 5778–5780 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d9093e838">17</a></sup>. Accordingly, further investigation of its pharmaceutical utility is severely limited by a shortage of supply. While suspension cultures, tissue cultures, and adventitious root cultures have been investigated as alternative sources of this bioactive diterpenoid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kutney, J. P. et al. Cyto-toxic diterpenes triptolide, tripdiolide, and cyto-toxic triterpenes from tissue-cultures of Tripterygium wilfordii. Can. J. Chem. 59, 2677–2683 (1981)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d9093e842">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Miao, G. P. et al. Elicitation and in situ adsorption enhanced secondary metabolites production of Tripterygium wilfordii Hook. f. adventitious root fragment liquid cultures in shake flask and a modified bubble column bioreactor. Bioprocess Biosyst. Eng. 37, 641–650 (2014)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d9093e842_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Su, P. et al. Characterization of eight terpenoids from tissue cultures of the Chinese herbal plant, Tripterygium wilfordii, by high-performance liquid chromatography coupled with electrospray ionization tandem mass spectrometry. Biomed. Chromatogr. 28, 1183–1192 (2014)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d9093e842_2">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Inabuy, F. S. et al. Biosynthesis of diterpenoids in Tripterygium adventitious root cultures. Plant Physiol. 175, 92–103 (2017)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d9093e842_3">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Miao, G. P. et al. Aggregate cell suspension cultures of Tripterygium wilfordii Hook. f. for triptolide, wilforgine, and wilforine production. Plant Cell Tissue Organ Cult. 112, 109–116 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d9093e845">22</a></sup>, a more promising approach to obtaining such structurally complex natural products is metabolic engineering. This can be attempted in the native plant or accomplished via a synthetic biology strategy, involving reconstitution in a suitably engineered microbial chassis organism, which can establish a sustainable and reliable means of production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Martin, V. J., Pitera, D. J., Withers, S. T., Newman, J. D. &amp; Keasling, J. D. Engineering a mevalonate pathway in Escherichia coli for production of terpenoids. Nat. Biotechnol. 21, 796–802 (2003)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d9093e849">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ro, D. K. et al. Production of the antimalarial drug precursor artemisinic acid in engineered yeast. Nature 440, 940–943 (2006)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d9093e849_1">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Paddon, C. J. et al. High-level semi-synthetic production of the potent antimalarial artemisinin. Nature 496, 528–532 (2013)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d9093e849_2">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Farhi, M. et al. Generation of the potent anti-malarial drug artemisinin in tobacco. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 1072–1074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d9093e852">26</a></sup>. However, this latter approach requires elucidation of the relevant biosynthetic pathway.</p><p>Triptolide is an abietane-type diterpenoid, produced via initial cyclization of (<i>E</i>,<i>E</i>,<i>E</i>)-geranylgeranyl diphosphate (GGPP) to copalyl diphosphate (CPP), with subsequent cyclization to the abietane-type diene olefin miltiradiene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Kutney, J. P. &amp; Han, K. Studies with plant-cell cultures of the Chinese herbal plant, Tripterygium wilfordii. Isolation and characterization of diterpenes. Recl. Trav. Chim. Pays-Bas 115, 77–93 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d9093e868">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Su, P. et al. Identification and functional characterization of diterpene synthases for triptolide biosynthesis from Tripterygium wilfordii. Plant J. 93, 50–65 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d9093e871">28</a></sup>. The 1,4-diene arrangement of the miltiradiene C ring leaves this poised for aromatization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Gao, W. et al. A functional genomics approach to tanshinone biosynthesis provides stereochemical insights. Org. Lett. 11, 5170–5173 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d9093e875">29</a></sup>, which most likely occurs spontaneously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Zi, J. &amp; Peters, R. J. Characterization of CYP76AH4 clarifies phenolic diterpenoid biosynthesis in the Lamiaceae. Org. Biomol. Chem. 11, 7650–7652 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d9093e880">30</a></sup>. Along with conversion of carbon-18 (C-18) from a methyl to carboxylic acid, this forms dehydroabietic acid, followed by oxidative 1,2-migration of C-18 (from C-4 → C-3) and further transformation to the phenolic triepoxide triptolide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Kutney, J. P. &amp; Han, K. Studies with plant-cell cultures of the Chinese herbal plant, Tripterygium wilfordii. Isolation and characterization of diterpenes. Recl. Trav. Chim. Pays-Bas 115, 77–93 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d9093e884">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Su, P. et al. Identification and functional characterization of diterpene synthases for triptolide biosynthesis from Tripterygium wilfordii. Plant J. 93, 50–65 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d9093e887">28</a></sup>. At present, elucidation of the triptolide pathway relies on transcriptomes, which has only led to identification of the relevant diterpene synthases, namely the relevant CPP synthase (CPS1) and miltiradiene synthase (MS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Su, P. et al. Identification and functional characterization of diterpene synthases for triptolide biosynthesis from Tripterygium wilfordii. Plant J. 93, 50–65 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d9093e891">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Hansen, N. L. et al. The terpene synthase gene family in Tripterygium wilfordii harbors a labdane-type diterpene synthase among the monoterpene synthase TPS-b subfamily. Plant J. 89, 429–441 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d9093e894">31</a></sup>, with subsequently acting enzymes, such as cytochrome P450s (CYPs), involved in further biosynthesis of the highly functionalized triptolide remaining enigmatic. CYPs form the largest family of enzymes in plants, playing manifold roles in their complex metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Nelson, D. &amp; Werck-Reichhart, D. A P450-centric view of plant evolution. Plant J. 66, 194–211 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d9093e898">32</a></sup>. Indeed, there are still CYP families, defined as phylogenetic clades with &lt;40% amino acid sequence identity between them<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Nelson, D. &amp; Werck-Reichhart, D. A P450-centric view of plant evolution. Plant J. 66, 194–211 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d9093e902">32</a></sup>, whose function remains unknown, with no biochemical activity yet assigned to any member<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Nelson, D. &amp; Werck-Reichhart, D. A P450-centric view of plant evolution. Plant J. 66, 194–211 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d9093e906">32</a></sup>. Recently, whole-genome sequencing provides a comprehensive genetic resource and has become a practical approach to not only elucidation of natural product biosynthetic pathways, but also insight into their evolution, as well as improvement of their production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Shen, Q. et al. The Genome of Artemisia annua provides insight into the evolution of Asteraceae family and artemisinin Biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 11, 776–788 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d9093e911">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Liu, X. et al. The genome of medicinal plant Macleaya cordata provides new insights into benzylisoquinoline alkaloids metabolism. Mol. Plant 10, 975–989 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d9093e914">34</a></sup>. Nevertheless, this must be coupled to other information directed more specifically at the natural product of interest.</p><p>Here we first present a high-quality reference-grade genome of <i>T. wilfordii</i> and show that duplications of triptolide biosynthetic pathway genes are almost all generated by a recent whole-genome triplication event. We then map a gene-to-metabolite network by integrating genomic, transcriptomic, and metabolomic data. Next, we combine the synthetic biology tools, RNAi knock-down, and overexpression to identify a cytochrome P450 (CYP728B70) that can catalyze oxidation of C-18 from a methyl to the acid moiety of dehydroabietic acid in triptolide biosynthesis. This work provides the genomic resource and the candidate genes that may contribute to fully elucidation of the triptolide biosynthetic pathway and consequently lead to heterologous bioproduction.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>Based on the k-mer distribution analysis, we estimated the genome size of <i>T. wilfordii</i> to be ~365.95 Mb with a high level of heterozygosity (1.95%) and repetition (48.87%), indicating the genome assembly was complicated (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">1</a>). The genome of <i>T. wilfordii</i> was sequenced using PacBio (read length of 60 kb, ~207.10× coverage) and 10X Genomics (~327.23× coverage) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">2</a>). The total length of the final assembly was 348.38 Mb with 467 contigs and a contig N50 of 4.36 Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">3</a>). Assessment of the completeness of the genome assembly with CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d9093e955">35</a></sup> indicated 96.77% coverage of the conserved core eukaryotic genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">5</a>), and BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d9093e962">36</a></sup> results indicated that the genome was 95.10% complete (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">6</a>). Additionally, 97.06% of the transcriptome can be mapped back to the assembly, further supporting a high level of genome coverage (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>The <i>T. wilfordii</i> assembly was further refined using high-throughput chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) data, comprised of 321 scaffolds with a scaffold N50 of 13.52 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Tab1">1</a>). As a result, 315.08 Mb of the assembly and 99.92% of the genes were distributed across 23 chromosome-level pseudomolecules (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig1">1a</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of <i>T. wilfordii</i> genome assembly and annotation.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genome evolution of T. wilfordii."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Genome evolution of <i>T. wilfordii</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14776-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14776_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14776-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14776_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="664" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Distribution of <i>T. wilfordii</i> genomic features. (I) Circular representation of the pseudomolecule. (II–IV) gene density (500 kb window), percentage of repeats (500 kb window), and GC content (500 kb window). (V) Each linking line in the center of the circle connects a pair of homologous genes. <b>b</b> Inferred phylogenetic tree with 514 single-copy genes of 15 plant species. Gene family expansions are indicated in green, and gene family contractions are indicated in red. The timing of whole-genome duplication (WGD) and the timing of whole-genome triplication (WGT) are superimposed on the tree. Divergence times are estimated by Maximum Likelihood (PAML). <b>c</b> Distribution of 4DTv shown in colored lines as indicated. <b>d</b> Syntenic dot plots show a 3–1 chromosomal relationship between <i>T. wilfordii</i> genome and <i>V. vinifera</i> genome. <b>e</b> Macrosynteny between <i>T. wilfordii</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> karyotypes. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We were able to annotate 28,321 protein-coding genes, with an average sequence length of 3338 bp, similar to those of other reported plants (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">9</a>). On average, each predicted gene contains 5.44 exons with an average sequence length of 228 bp. A total of 182.52 Mb of repetitive elements occupying 52.36% of the <i>T. wilfordii</i> genome were annotated (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">2</a>). The majority of the repeats are long terminal repeats (LTRs) (34.26% of the genome; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">10</a>). Approximately 99.6% of the genes were functionally annotated by similarity searches against homologous sequences and protein domains (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">11</a>). In addition, we identified noncoding RNA (ncRNA) genes, including 2,563 rRNA, 407 tRNA, 373 miRNA, and 892 snRNA genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">12</a>). These results further support the completeness of our <i>T. wilfordii</i> genome sequence and a schematic representation of the genome is given in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig1">1a</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome evolution contributed to formation of triptolide</h3><p>To investigate the evolution of <i>T. wilfordii</i>, we constructed a phylogenetic tree and estimated the divergence times of 15 plant species using 514 single-copy genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig1">1b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">3</a>). Phylogenomic analysis showed that <i>T. wilfordii</i> was most related to the ancestor of <i>Manihot esculenta</i> and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, with an estimated divergence time of 87.1 million years ago (MYA). Gene family expansion and contraction was examined using CAFÉ (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">3</a>). Among the <i>T. wilfordii, P. trichocarpa, M. esculenta</i>, and <i>Cephalotus follicularis</i> gene families, a total of 951 genes appeared unique to <i>T. wilfordii</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">5</a>). Interestingly, Gene Ontology (GO) and Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) analyses found these <i>T. wilfordii</i>-specific genes were particularly enriched in the terms terpene synthase, oxidation-reduction process, and plant-pathogen interaction (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><p>The distribution of 4DTv (fourfold degenerate synonymous sites of the third codons) of all gene pairs found in each segment showed two peaks at approximately 0.09 and 0.48 in the <i>T. wilfordii</i> genome. The first peak at approximately 0.48 revealed the core eudicot γ triplication event, and the second peak at approximately 0.09 indicated that <i>T. wilfordii</i> underwent another whole-genome multiplication event after diverging from <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>M. esculenta</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig1">1c</a>). We further compared <i>T. wilfordii</i> genome and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> genome. 77% of <i>T. wilfordii</i> gene models are in syntenic blocks corresponding to one <i>V. vinifera</i> region, covering 83% of the <i>V. vinifera</i> gene space, among which 35% have three orthologous regions in <i>T. wilfordii</i>, 31% have two, and 15% have one (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">6</a>). Intergenomic co-linearity analysis was consistent with both the γ-event and another, more specific WGT event for <i>T. wilfordii</i>, as indicated by a 1:3 syntenic relationship between <i>T. wilfordii</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig1">1d, e</a>). The recent WGT event was dated to approximately 21 ± 6 MYA, as indicated by the distribution of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i>S) of syntenic genes in <i>T. wilfordii</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">7</a>). The recent WGT event occurred in the Paleogene (65–23.3 MYA) to Neogene (23.3-1.64 MYA) period, which may have enabled <i>T. wilfordii</i> to cope better with the markedly changed environment by functional redundancy, mutational robustness, increased evolution rate, and adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Crow, K. D. &amp; Wagner, G. P. Investigators ST-NY. Proceedings of the SMBE Tri-National Young Investigators’ Workshop 2005. What is the role of genome duplication in the evolution of complexity and diversity? Mol. Biol. Evol. 23, 887–892 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d9093e1469">37</a></sup>.</p><p>Gene families that may be involved in terpenoid (e.g., triptolide in <i>T. wilfordii</i>) biosynthesis were identified in the fifteen reported plant species. The results showed that the copy number of these gene families varied among all examined plant species, with those encoding <i>DXS</i>, <i>GGPPS</i>, <i>TPS</i> and <i>CYP</i> exhibiting particularly strong variation (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">18</a>). To further investigate the role of WGT events on triptolide biosynthesis, we carried out phylogenetic analysis of the gene families potentially involved in this pathway, specifically <i>Ks</i> calculations for each duplicated gene pair, including those from upstream isoprenoid metabolism (i.e., <i>ACAT</i>, <i>CMK</i>, <i>DXS</i>, <i>FPS</i>, <i>GPS</i>, <i>GGPPS</i>, <i>HDR</i>, <i>HDS</i>, <i>HMGR</i>, <i>HMGS</i>, <i>IDI</i>, <i>MCT</i>, <i>MVK</i>, <i>MVD</i>, <i>PMK</i>) and those more specific to triptolide (i.e., <i>CPS</i> and <i>MS</i>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">19</a>). We found that duplications of these genes were almost all generated by the recent WGT event (i.e., <i>ACAT</i>, <i>CMK</i>, <i>GPS</i>, <i>HDR</i>, <i>HMGS</i>, <i>IDI</i>, <i>MVK</i>, <i>MVD</i>, <i>PMK</i>, <i>CPS</i> and <i>MS</i>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">9</a>), suggesting that the recent WGT event were important to the evolution of triptolide biosynthesis in <i>T. wilfordii</i>.</p><p>Notably it was found that the <i>TwCPS1</i> and <i>TwMS</i> genes already known to be involved in triptolide biosynthesis are adjacent to each other in the <i>T. wilfordii</i> genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">10</a>), which is similar to previously identified biosynthetic gene clusters in other plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nutzmann, H. W. &amp; Osbourn, A. Gene clustering in plant specialized metabolism. Curr. Opin. Biotechnol. 26, 91–99 (2014)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d9093e1614">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Guo, L. et al. The opium poppy genome and morphinan production. Science 362, 343–347 (2018)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d9093e1614_1">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Shang, Y. et al. Biosynthesis, regulation, and domestication of bitterness in cucumber. Science 346, 1084–1088 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d9093e1617">40</a></sup>. However, while the relevant region on chromosome 21 also contains a <i>CYP</i> (TW023804.1) that exhibits a similar expression pattern, this is sufficiently distant, including a number of intervening genes clearly unrelated to triptolide biosynthesis, to leave its relevance unclear. Similarly, while there are several nearby transcription factors (TFs), as well as another CPS, these are unlikely to play a role in the production of triptolide.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Integrated transcriptome and metabolome analysis</h3><p>To gain further insights into triptolide biosynthesis, as well as the organization and regulation of the triptolide pathway, suspension cells induced by methyl jasmonate (MeJA) and seven different tissues were used as the source of transcripts and metabolites (Supplementary Notes <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">5</a>). The results showed that triptolide levels in MeJA-induced cells were 3.6-fold higher (relative to control cultures) after 360 h, while the levels of triptophenolide were even higher, 55-fold (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig2">2</a>). More detailed analysis was focused on a final set of 142 peaks with a mass ratio of 295–400, the expected range for diterpenoids (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">11</a>-<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">14</a> and Supplementary Note 7). For example, the content of these potentially triptolide-related metabolites was highest in the root bark (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">16</a>). Data sets for these accumulated metabolites and gene expression (including all <i>CYPs</i>, <i>TwCPS1</i> and <i>TwMS</i>) were separately normalized, and correlation network analysis was used to establish gene-to-metabolite coregulation patterns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Mounet, F. et al. Gene and metabolite regulatory network analysis of early developing fruit tissues highlights new candidate genes for the control of tomato fruit composition and development. Plant Physiol. 149, 1505–1528 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d9093e1664">41</a></sup> in suspension cells and the various tissues, respectively. The Pearson correlation coefficient between each set of variables (either metabolite or gene) was calculated, including all conditions and time points (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">4</a>). The correlation network was analyzed by Cytoscape (version 3.6.1), using a correlation coefficient &gt;0.7 as the cutoff (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">20</a>). The utility of the gene-to-metabolite network was verified by the observation that the key genes <i>TwCPS1</i> and <i>TwMS</i> were both strongly associated with triptolide and triptophenolide. Accordingly, we proceeded under the assumption that the CYP catalyzing the next step to form dehydroabietic acid should similarly be present among such strongly related genes. Indeed, a total of 97 <i>CYP</i> genes, of which 57 genes were reasonably well-expressed—i.e., with RPKM &gt; 1 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">21</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">6</a>)—were strongly associated with triptolide in the network. These then are potentially involved in triptolide biosynthesis.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparative transcriptomic analysis of genes involved in the triptolide biosynthetic pathway."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Comparative transcriptomic analysis of genes involved in the triptolide biosynthetic pathway.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14776-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14776_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14776-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14776_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="622" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Suspension cells of <i>T. wilfordii</i> were treated with methyl jasmonate and DMSO. 0, 4, 12, 24, 48, 72, 120, 240 represent the time points of each sample, CC means the control group of cells, and CM means the group of MJ-treated cells. Heat maps of these genes were plotted using MeV software (version 4.9.0). Error bars, mean ± SD (<i>n</i> = 3 biologically independent samples; * <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, **<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01, ***<i>P</i> &lt; 0.001 by 2-sided Student’s <i>t</i> test). Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Identification of specific <i>CYP</i> genes in <i>T. wilfordii</i>
</h3><p>So far, triptolide is found only in <i>Tripterygium</i>, indicating that some of the CYPs involved in the biosynthesis of triptolide may be specific to <i>T. wilfordii</i>. In order to identify such species-specific CYPs, we annotated a total of 2335 <i>CYP</i> genes in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Daucus carota, Glycyrrhiza uralensis, Prunus persica, P. trichocarpa, Solanum lycopersicum, Salvia miltiorrhiza</i> and <i>T. wilfordii</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">18</a>). We constructed a phylogenetic tree from amino acid sequence alignment of all these CYPs and identified <i>T. wilfordii</i> specific CYPs using a cutoff of 55% identity, which indicates separate sub-family assignment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Liu, X. et al. Engineering yeast for the production of breviscapine by genomic analysis and synthetic biology approaches. Nat. Commun. 9, 448 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d9093e1773">42</a></sup>. This revealed 22 <i>T. wilfordii</i>-specific <i>CYP</i> genes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">5</a>). Interestingly, expression levels of six of these <i>CYPs</i> were significantly increased by MeJA induction, and most of these were highly expressed in root bark in which the terpene synthase genes were enriched and most of them were highly expressed (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">22</a>), leading to strong correlation with triptolide and/or triptophenolide.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Functional identification of CYP728B70</h3><p>Among <i>CYP</i> genes correlated with triptolide in the gene-to-metabolite network, as well as those specific to <i>T. wilfordii</i>, there were 13 found to express differentially between MeJA-induced and control cells at 4, 12, 24, 48, and 72 h, and/or express differentially between root bark and other tissues (e.g., flower, stem bark, peeled stem, and leaf), and also exhibit highly similar expression patterns as <i>TwCPS1</i> and <i>TwMS</i>, including high expression levels in root bark (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">18</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">6</a>). Among these candidates, TW016590.1 was already previously identified as <i>ent</i>-kaurene oxidase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Su, P. et al. Probing the single key amino acid responsible for the novel catalytic function of ent-kaurene oxidase supported by NADPH-cytochrome P450 reductases in Tripterygium wilfordii. Front Plant Sci. 8, 1756 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d9093e1833">43</a></sup>, TW012756.1 was annotated as trans-cinnamate 4-monooxygenase, which is not likely to be involved in terpenoid biosynthesis, while TW017699.1 and TW013472.1 appeared to arise from a relatively recent tandem gene duplication event as their amino acid sequences are 97% identical (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">2</a>), and we chose to only target TW017699.1 directly. Accordingly, we settled on a total of 10 candidates for the follow-up RNAi studies, which were carried out with suspension cell cultures to provide more direct evidence for a role in triptolide biosynthesis for these 10 candidates (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">20</a>). In 4 RNAi-lines (CYP728B70, TW011445.1, TW012149.1, TW006625.1), the transcript levels of the targeted gene and triptolide accumulation were decreased compared to those in control cultures transformed with an empty vector (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig3">3b</a>). These four CYPs were co-expressed with the cytochrome P450 reductase 3 (CPR3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Su, P. et al. Probing the single key amino acid responsible for the novel catalytic function of ent-kaurene oxidase supported by NADPH-cytochrome P450 reductases in Tripterygium wilfordii. Front Plant Sci. 8, 1756 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d9093e1847">43</a></sup> in yeast also engineered to produce miltiradiene, as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Dai, Z., Liu, Y., Huang, L. &amp; Zhang, X. Production of miltiradiene by metabolically engineered Saccharomyces cerevisiae. Biotechnol. Bioeng. 109, 2845–2853 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d9093e1851">44</a></sup>. Gratifyingly, with CYP728B70, which was strongly associated with triptolide in the gene-metabolite network, this led to the formation of four compounds, dehydroabietic acid (abieta-8,11,13-trien-18-oic acid, <b>1</b>), miltiradienoic acid (abieta-8,12-dien-18-oic acid, <b>2</b>), dehydroabietinol (abieta-8,11,13-trien-18-ol, <b>3</b>) and miltiradienol (abieta-8,12-dien-18-ol, <b>4</b>), whose structures were identified after purification by <sup>1</sup>H NMR and <sup>13</sup>C NMR (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig3">3c</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">24</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">32</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">6</a>). To further verify such activity, in vivo assays also were performed, that is, substrate feeding of cultures expressing CYP728B70 in the yeast WAT11 strain that also expresses an Arabidopsis CPR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Urban, P., Mignotte, C., Kazmaier, M., Delorme, F. &amp; Pompon, D. Cloning, yeast expression, and characterization of the coupling of two distantly related Arabidopsis thaliana NADPH-cytochrome P450 reductases with P450 CYP73A5. J. Biol. Chem. 272, 19176–19186 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d9093e1885">45</a></sup>. Compounds <b>1</b>-<b>4</b> were detected when feeding miltiradiene (abieta-8,12-diene), while only the derived compounds <b>1</b> and <b>3</b> were detected when feeding abieta-8,11,13-triene. Similarly, compounds <b>1</b> and <b>2</b> were detected when feeding compound <b>4</b> (miltiradienol), while only compound <b>1</b> was detected when feeding compound <b>3</b> (dehydroabietinol) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig3">3d, e</a>). Given that aromatization of the miltiradiene C ring to form abietatriene most likely occurs spontaneously, these results indicate that TwCYP728B70 catalyzes consecutive oxidations at C-18 of miltiradiene or abietatriene to form the corresponding alcohol and acid derivatives. Although the expected aldehyde intermediate is not observed, this almost certainly also is formed between the alcohol and acid final products.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="CYP gene screening and functional identification of CYP728B70."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: <i>CYP</i> gene screening and functional identification of CYP728B70.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14776-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14776_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14776-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14776_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="738" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Hierarchical clustering of RNA-Seq expression data after filtering by expression level. <b>b</b> Relative expression of 10 candidate CYPs and triptolide concentration in RNAi suspension cells. <b>c</b> Co-expression of CYP728B70 and TwCPR3 in miltiradiene engineering yeast. <b>d</b> In vivo transformation of authentic diterpene alcohol substrates to the corresponding alcohols and acids in cultures of yeast cells expressing CYP728B70. <b>e</b> In vivo
