Error bars, mean ± SD (<i>n</i> = 3 biologically independent samples; *<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, **<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01 by 2-sided Student’s <i>t</i> test). Source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM6">3a, b, f</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further explore the role of CYP728B70 in triptolide biosynthesis, overexpression was employed. This led to a significant increase in transcript level, albeit only by ~1.6-fold compared with the control cultures transformed with the empty vector, on the 3rd day, and it then decreased but was still slightly higher than in the control cultures at later time points. Nevertheless, elevated levels of triptolide in the overexpression line relative to those in the control cultures were evident on the 9th day, reaching just over 30.5 μg g<sup>−1</sup> in the overexpression line, representing an ~70% increase (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig3">3f</a>). This not only further supports a role for CYP720B70 in triptolide biosynthesis, but also showcases its potential utility for improving production of this valuable diterpenoid in <i>T. wilfordii</i>.</p><p>To explore the potential functions of the other 9 candidate CYPs, in vitro enzymatic activity assays were performed using the available dehydroabietic acid, triptinin B, triptophenolide and triptoquinonide, all of which are likely intermediates in triptolide biosynthesis (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">7</a>). However, no new compounds were observed, indicating that these CYPs could not catalyze transformation of these intermediates, but does not rule out a role for them in triptolide biosynthesis (i.e., in mediating reactions involving other intermediates) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">33</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Triptolide, a structurally complex phenolic diterpene triepoxide of <i>T. wilfordii</i>, has potent antitumor and immunosuppressive activities. Research focusing on its biosynthetic pathway has been stymied, in part, by the absence of a comprehensive genetic accounting. The high-quality reference-grade genome of <i>T. wilfordii</i> reported here thus provides a valuable genomic resource for investigation of triptolide biosynthesis, and is, to the best of our knowledge, the first genomic sequence for a plant of the order Celastrales. Accordingly, it further represents a cornerstone for evolutionary phylogenomic studies of not only <i>T. wilfordii</i>, but the Celastrales order more generally.</p><p>Previous investigations of triptolide biosynthesis relied on transcriptome data, and only identified the relevant diterpene synthases. Herein, we integrated genomic, transcriptomic, and metabolomic data to map a gene-to-metabolite network, screening out 57 <i>CYP</i> genes which were potentially involved in triptolide biosynthesis. Then we combined the co-expression patterns, tissue-specificity and inducibility of these candidate genes as well as <i>T. wilfordii-</i>specific <i>CYP</i> genes for further investigations, resulting in a total of 10 candidates. Through the above analysis, we identified 4 <i>CYP</i> genes involved in triptolide biosynthesis, as RNAi knock-down of these led to decreased accumulation of this natural product. Moreover, we successfully characterized the role of CYP728B70, which is responsible for oxidation of C-18 from a methyl to the acid moiety of dehydroabietic acid, in triptolide biosynthesis. As demonstrated here by substrate feeding studies, CYP728B70 exhibits multiple/sequential reactivity in carrying out this transformation in triptolide biosynthesis. Consistent with the limited accumulation of triptolide in <i>T. wilfordii</i>, <i>CYP728B70</i> exhibits low transcript levels, although it is highest in the root bark where this diterpenoid is primarily found. Notably, overexpression of <i>CYP728B70</i> in plant cell cultures led to a significant (~70%) increase in triptolide levels relative to control cultures, indicating that CYP728B70 activity limits triptolide biosynthesis, and demonstrating the utility of the results reported here for improving the yield of this potential pharmaceutical agent.</p><p>Perhaps not surprisingly given that CYPs form the largest family of enzymes, there are still CYP families that are completely uncharacterized. Indeed, this included the CYP728 family, which was first observed in <i>Amborella</i>, although it has been lost in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">35</a>), and no function has been previously reported for any member of this CYP family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Nelson, D. &amp; Werck-Reichhart, D. A P450-centric view of plant evolution. Plant J. 66, 194–211 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2055">32</a></sup>. Hence, CYP728B70 appears to be the only one member of this CYP family whose catalytic activity has been identified, and its role in diterpenoid metabolism immediately suggests similar function for other family members. Also, the stepwise carboxylation reaction observed for CYP728B70 is catalytically highly similar to the CYP720B family in conifers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Ro, D. K., Arimura, G., Lau, S. Y., Piers, E. &amp; Bohlmann, J. Loblolly pine abietadienol/abietadienal oxidase PtAO (CYP720B1) is a multifunctional, multisubstrate cytochrome P450 monooxygenase. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 8060–8065 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2059">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Hamberger, B., Ohnishi, T., Hamberger, B., Seguin, A. &amp; Bohlmann, J. Evolution of diterpene metabolism: Sitka spruce CYP720B4 catalyzes multiple oxidations in resin acid biosynthesis of conifer defense against insects. Plant Physiol. 157, 1677–1695 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2062">47</a></sup> that even forms some common products, thus showcasing an intriguing case of independent evolution of these functions in distant plant species. In addition, the other 3 <i>CYP</i> genes that affect the biosynthesis of triptolide in the results of RNAi experiment are from <i>T. wilfordii</i>-specific CYP (sub-)families. Although we have not identified their catalytic functions, such biochemical characterization will then provide similar insight.</p><p>Although some steps in the triptolide biosynthetic pathway still remain unknown, we have provided many promising candidates, including <i>CYP</i> genes and metabolites that might constitute yet-unknown intermediates or side products from triptolide biosynthesis. We have also provided many potential TFs that are most likely involved in the regulation of the biosynthesis of triptolide (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">34</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">8</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">13</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">8</a>). Of particular note here is the identification of roles for CYPs from (sub-)families that are specific to <i>T. wilfordii</i>, as much of the reported work in such characterization of CYP function in plant natural products biosynthesis has to some extent relied on homology at least at the family, if not sub-family, level.</p><p>In addition to our identification of the role of CYP728B70, we used this to engineer yeast for the production of dehydroabietic acid (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig4">4</a>). This lays the foundation for identification of genes encoding subsequently acting enzymes, and will be invaluable in future investigations of triptolide biosynthesis.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Analysis of triptolide pathway and metabolic engineering."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Analysis of triptolide pathway and metabolic engineering.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14776-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14776_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-14776-1/MediaObjects/41467_2020_14776_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="663" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Three reactions presumably catalyzed by CYP728B70 to convert miltiradiene to dehydroabietic acid. <b>b</b> Establishing the metabolic pathways in yeast for production of diterpene alcohols and acids. In module 1, <i>rox1</i>, <i>ypl062w</i>, <i>yjl06w4</i> were knocked out, and <i>ERG9</i> was down-regulated, and <i>tHMG1</i>, <i>ERG20</i>, <i>BTS1</i>, <i>SaGGPPS</i> were overexpressed to improve the production of GGPP. In module 2, double SmMS-SmCPS1 fusion modules were introduced into the yeast chromosome for the production of miltiradiene. Co-expression of TwCYP728B70 and TwCPR3 leads to the observed production of the derived alcohols and acids.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In conclusion, while it is difficult to resolve the biosynthetic pathways of the complex natural products in non-model systems, we have demonstrated here the utility of the multi-omics data, as well as co-expression patterns, tissue-specificity, and inducibility of candidate genes will contribute for such investigations. Interestingly, while genome sequencing found pairing of the genes for the consecutively acting <i>TwCPS1</i> and <i>TwMS</i> can initiate triptolide biosynthesis, these do not appear to have be co-clustered with genes for later acting enzymes. Nevertheless, the genome sequence reported here provides a comprehensive genetic inventory, which was coupled with the observation that triptolide production is tissue-specific and affected by elicitors, much as found with other plant natural products<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Goossens, A. It is easy to get huge candidate gene lists for plant metabolism now, but how to get beyond? Mol. Plant 8, 2–5 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2161">48</a></sup>, to generate gene-to-metabolite networks that can be productively mined. Notably, this approach has led to identification of four relevant CYPs, with the characterized function of CYP728B70 further providing insight into this previously enigmatic CYP family, as well as demonstrating the utility of this approach to increasing the yield of this promising pharmaceutical agent.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>A <i>T. wilfordii</i> cultivar was used for sequencing (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">1</a>). Genomic DNA was extracted from leaves of <i>T. wilfordii</i> using the DNAsecure Plant Kit (TIANGEN) and broken into random fragments. Short-reads libraries were constructed according to the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina, San Diego, CA) and then sequenced on Illumina Hiseq X-ten. For long-read DNA sequencing, 60 kb Single Molecule Real Time (SMRT) long-read library were sequenced on the PacBio Sequel platform (75.79 Gb data, 207-fold coverage of the genome). For 10X Genomics sequencing, a total of 119.75 Gb (327-fold coverage of the genome) data were sequenced on the Illumina Hiseq X-Ten (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><p>De novo assembly of the long reads from the PacBio SMRT Sequencer was performed using FALCON (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2199">49</a></sup>. To obtain enough corrected reads, the longest coverage of subreads were firstly selected as seed reads to correct sequence errors. Then, error-corrected reads were aligned to each other and assembled into genomic contigs using FALCON with the following parameters: length_cutoff_pr = 10,000, max_diff = 95, and max_cov = 105. Then, genomic contigs were polished using Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2203">50</a></sup>, which yielded an assembly with a contig N50 size of 4.36 Mb. The total length of this assembly version was 348.38 Mb. Then, we used BWA-MEM to align the 10X Genomics data to the assembly using default settings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Li, H. Toward better understanding of artifacts in variant calling from high-coverage samples. Bioinformatics 30, 2843–2851 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2207">51</a></sup>. Scaffolding was performed by FragScaff with the barcoded sequencing reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Adey, A. et al. In vitro, long-range sequence information for de novo genome assembly via transposase contiguity. Genome Res. 24, 2041–2049 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2211">52</a></sup>. Last, Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2216">53</a></sup> was used to perform error correction based on the Illumina sequences, generating a genome with a scaffold N50 size of 6.48 Mb. The total length of this assembly version was 349.91 Mb. Subsequently, the Hi-C sequencing data were aligned to the assembled scaffolds by BWA-mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2220">50</a></sup> and the scaffolds were clustered onto chromosomes with LACHESIS (<a href="http://shendurelab.github.io/LACHESIS/">http://shendurelab.github.io/LACHESIS/</a>), the final genome was 348.53 Mb and the contig and scaffold N50 were 4.36 Mb and 13.52 Mb, respectively (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">3</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome annotation</h3><p>A total of 52.36% repeat sequences in the genome were annotated. Among them, TEs were searched by combining de novo-based and homology-based approaches using RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>), LTR_FINDER (<a href="http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder/">http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder/</a>), RepeatScout (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>), RepeatMasker (version 3.3.0) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>), and RepeatProteinMask (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>). Tandem repeats were detected using Tandem Repeats Finder (TRF)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2284">54</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">10</a>). Gene structures were predicted with a combination of homology-based prediction, de novo prediction and transcriptome-based prediction in the genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">9</a>). We then generated functional assignments of the <i>T. wilfordii</i> genes with BLAST in public protein databases, including SwissProt (<a href="https://web.expasy.org/docs/swiss-prot_guideline.html">https://web.expasy.org/docs/swiss-prot_guideline.html</a>), NR, InterPro (V32.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D211–215 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2314">55</a></sup>, Pfam (V27.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Finn, R. D. et al. Pfam: the protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D222–230 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2318">56</a></sup> and KEGG (<a href="https://www.kegg.jp/">https://www.kegg.jp/</a>). Finally, 99.6% of all genes in the genome were predicted to be functional (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">11</a>). Noncoding RNA was predicted using de novo and homology search methods (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome evolution</h3><p>We conducted expansion and contraction analysis using the CAFÉ program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W. C., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987–1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2347">57</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">15</a>) and identified the positively selected genes in <i>T. wilfordii</i> using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2357">58</a></sup> (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">17</a>). To identify the WGD events in the <i>T. wilfordii</i> genome, we used McscanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Tang, H. B. et al. Perspective—synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2371">59</a></sup> to determine syntenic blocks (regions with at least five genes) and calculated 4DTv for all gene pairs found in each syntenic segment. The synonymous substitution rate (<i>Ks</i>) values of <i>T. wilfordii</i> syntenic block genes were calculated with the codeml program of the PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2381">60</a></sup> package. We performed synteny analysis on <i>T. wilfordii</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> to confirm that <i>T. wilfordii</i> had undergone another WGT event.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Evolution of triptolide biosynthesis genes</h3><p>To investigate the genes involved in triptolide biosynthesis, we first retrieved protein sequences from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> genes, including <i>ACAT</i>, <i>CMK, DXR</i>, <i>DXS</i>, <i>FPS</i>, <i>GGPPS</i>, <i>GPS</i>, <i>HDR</i>, <i>HDS</i>, <i>HMGR</i>, <i>HMGS</i>, <i>IDI</i>, <i>MCT</i>, <i>MCS</i>, <i>MVK</i>, <i>MVD,</i> and <i>PMK</i> from the NCBI database. The CPS and MS protein sequences in <i>T. wilfordii</i> were previously cloned<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Su, P. et al. Identification and functional characterization of diterpene synthases for triptolide biosynthesis from Tripterygium wilfordii. Plant J. 93, 50–65 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2460">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Hansen, N. L. et al. The terpene synthase gene family in Tripterygium wilfordii harbors a labdane-type diterpene synthase among the monoterpene synthase TPS-b subfamily. Plant J. 89, 429–441 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2463">31</a></sup>. Then, using these homologs as queries, we identified the candidates in <i>T. wilfordii</i> and 13 other plant species, including <i>Carica papaya, Daucus carota, Dimocarpus longan, Glycine max, Gossypium raimondii, Glycyrrhiza uralensis, Oryza sativa, Prunus persica, Populus trichocarpa, Solanum lycopersicum, Salvia miltiorrhiza, Theobroma cacao, Vitis vinifera</i> using BLASTP with an E-value cutoff of 1e<sup>−5</sup>. The aligned hits with at least 50% coverage of seed protein sequences and &gt;50% protein sequence identity were selected as homologs. Then, the domains of these homologs were predicted by PFAM (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/">http://pfam.xfam.org/</a>). Only genes that had the same protein domain were considered to be homologs. The TPS and CYP450 genes were predicted using hmmsearch<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Finn, R. D., Clements, J. &amp; Eddy, S. R. HMMER web server: interactive sequence similarity searching. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, W29–W37 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2483">61</a></sup> in conjunction with the TPS hmm model (PF01397 and PF03936) and CYP450 hmm model (PF00067) from Pfam, respectively. We constructed phylogenetic trees of each identified triptolide biosynthetic gene family from <i>T. wilfordii</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. Amino acid sequence alignments of the identified triptolide biosynthetic genes from <i>T. wilfordii</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> were performed using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2499">58</a></sup>, and the alignment data were used to construct phylogenetic trees using RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2504">62</a></sup> with the maximum likelihood method. The timing of the divergence of duplicates in each triptolide biosynthesis gene was estimated based on the calculation of the synonymous substitution rate (Ks) using the codeml program of the PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2508">60</a></sup> package. The calculated <i>Ks</i> value was then converted to the divergence time according to <i>T</i> = <i>Ks</i>/2r, where r represents a substitution rate of 6.5 × 10<sup>−9</sup> mutations per site per year for eudicots (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">19</a>).These gene pairs were not adjacent in the distribution of the genome (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Suspension cell elicitation and sample preparation</h3><p>It has previously been shown that the application of the plant defence signaling molecule MeJA could increase terpenoid production and affect the transcript levels of the genes involved in the related pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Su, P. et al. Identification and functional characterization of diterpene synthases for triptolide biosynthesis from Tripterygium wilfordii. Plant J. 93, 50–65 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2539">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Rischer, H. et al. Gene-to-metabolite networks for terpenoid indole alkaloid biosynthesis in Catharanthus roseus cells. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 5614–5619 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2542">63</a></sup>. Cell samples were harvested at 0, 4, 8, 12, 24, 36, 48, 72, 96, 120, 240, 360, and 480 h after the addition of MeJA and DMSO. Solid samples were homogenized by Mixer Mill MM 400 of Retsch (Retsch GmbH, 42781 Haan, Germany) and stored at −80 °C for at least 4 h prior to freeze drying for 48 h (Alpha 1-2 LDplus, Germany). For each sample, 60 mg aliquots were soaked in 1.5 mL of 80% (v/v) methanol overnight at room temperature and then dissolved in an ultrasonic water bath for 60 min. The supernatant was filtered through a 0.22-μm membrane filter (FitMax Syringe Filter, 13 mm 0.22 μm) for further analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">UPLC/Q-TOF MS analysis</h3><p>All analyses were performed on a UPLC/Q-TOF MS system (Waters Corp., Milford, MA, USA). The UPLC separation was performed using a Waters ACQUITY UPLC HSS T3 analytical column (2.1 mm × 100 mm, 1.8 μm) kept at 40 °C and a Waters Acquity UPLC<sup>TM</sup> I-Class system. The mobile phase was pumped with a mixture of 0.1% (v/v) aqueous acetic acid (A) and acetonitrile (B) at a flow rate of 0.5 mL min<sup>−1</sup>. Gradient elution was performed as follows: 0 min at 30% B, 6 min at 45% B, 18 min at 60% B, and 23 min at 90% B. The TOF MS experiments were performed on the Xevo G2-S QTOF MS system. The experiment was performed in ESI (+) ionization modes, and the data acquisition modes were MSE continuum. The capillary voltage was 0.5 KV, and the cone voltage was 40 V. The source and desolvation temperatures were 100 °C and 450 °C, respectively. The desolvation gas flow was 900 L h<sup>−1</sup>. The ramp collision energy was set as 20–40 eV for the high-energy scans. The MS range of data acquisition was 50–1500 Da. A lock spray with leucine enkephalin (200 pg μL<sup>−1</sup>, 10 μL min<sup>−1</sup>) was used as the reference (m/z 556.2771 ESI (+)) to maintain the mass accuracy.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Data analysis of metabolic and transcriptional profiles</h3><p>All mass spectral data were imported into Progenesis QI for data processing, then were grouped and exported to Ezinfo for principal component analysis (PCA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Ghosson, H., Schwarzenberg, A., Jamois, F. &amp; Yvin, J. C. Simultaneous untargeted and targeted metabolomics profiling of underivatized primary metabolites in sulfur-deficient barley by ultra-high performance liquid chromatography-quadrupole/time-of-flight mass spectrometry. Plant Methods 14, 62 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2572">64</a></sup>. PCA is a very useful statistical method for defining the dimensions of large data sets and identifying significant signals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Fukusaki, E. &amp; Kobayashi, A. Plant metabolomics: potential for practical operation. J. Biosci. Bioeng. 100, 347–354 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2576">65</a></sup>. The data set was log normalized, and PCA and OPLS-DA analyses were performed to determine overall differences in metabolites. An ANOVA with a significance level of <i>P</i> &lt; 0.01 and max-fold &gt; 2 was subsequently performed on the doubly filtered peaks to identify metabolites that did not change significantly in response to MeJA treatment. Furthermore, peaks with no fragments were removed.</p><p>We selected metabolite peaks and genes including all CYP, CPS1 and MS for network regulation analysis. Both data sets of accumulation of metabolic and gene expression were normalized separately, and correlation network analysis was used to establish gene-to-metabolite coregulation patterns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Mounet, F. et al. Gene and metabolite regulatory network analysis of early developing fruit tissues highlights new candidate genes for the control of tomato fruit composition and development. Plant Physiol. 149, 1505–1528 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2586">41</a></sup>. The Pearson correlation coefficient was calculated by the PCC method in the R platform between each set of variables (either metabolite or gene) across the profiles, and significant positive correlations with <i>p</i>-value &lt; 0.05 were detected between genes and metabolites. The correlation network was analyzed by the Cytoscape software (version 3.6.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Shannon, P. et al. Cytoscape: asoftware environment for integrated models of biomolecular interaction networks. Genome Res 13, 2498–2504 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2593">66</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Construction of miltiradiene-producing yeast strain</h3><p>To construct a miltiradiene-producing strain, the corresponding biosynthetic pathway was introduced into yeast. First, the CRISPR/Cas9 system was applied to improve the MVA pathway flux by knocking out three transcriptional regulators (<i>rox1</i>, <i>ypl062w</i> and <i>yjl06w4</i>) and knocking down <i>erg9</i> in the yeast BY-T20 strain (BY4742, <i>ΔTrp1, Trp1::His3-P</i><sub><i>PGK1</i></sub><i>-BTS1/ERG20-T</i><sub><i>ADH1</i></sub><i>-P</i><sub><i>TDH3</i></sub><i>-SaGGPPS-T</i><sub><i>TPI1</i></sub><i>-P</i><sub><i>TEF1</i></sub><i>-tHMG1-T</i><sub><i>CYC1</i></sub>), generating a GGPP-producing yeast strain named BY-HZ16<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Dai, Z., Liu, Y., Huang, L. &amp; Zhang, X. Production of miltiradiene by metabolically engineered Saccharomyces cerevisiae. Biotechnol. Bioeng. 109, 2845–2853 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2650">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dai, Z. et al. Metabolic engineering of Saccharomyces cerevisiae for production of ginsenosides. Metab. Eng. 20, 146–156 (2013)." href="#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2653">67</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ozaydin, B., Burd, H., Lee, T. S. &amp; Keasling, J. D. Carotenoid-based phenotypic screen of the yeast deletion collection reveals new genes with roles in isoprenoid production. Metab. Eng. 15, 174–183 (2013)." href="#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2653_1">68</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Jakociunas, T. et al. Multiplex metabolic pathway engineering using CRISPR/Cas9 in Saccharomyces cerevisiae. Metab. Eng. 28, 213–222 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2656">69</a></sup>.</p><p>The fusion of <i>SmCPS1</i> and <i>SmMS</i> from <i>Salvia miltiorrhiza</i> was reported to possess great potential to synthesize miltiradiene via GGPP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Zhou, Y. J. et al. Modular pathway engineering of diterpenoid synthases and the mevalonic acid pathway for miltiradiene production. J. Am. Chem. Soc. 134, 3234–3241 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2672">70</a></sup>. The single or double <i>SmMS-SmCPS1</i> fusion module was cloned and integrated into the yeast chromosome by the M2S integration method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Li, S., Ding, W., Zhang, X., Jiang, H. &amp; Bi, C. Development of a modularized two-step (M2S) chromosome integration technique for integration of multiple transcription units in Saccharomyces cerevisiae. Biotechnol. Biofuels 9, 232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2680">71</a></sup>. Briefly, <i>SmMS-SmCPS1</i> was amplified with the addition of a <i>Bsa</i>I digestion site and ligated with head-to-head promoters (<i>pTDH3-pADH1</i>) into the bi-terminator vector T1-(<i>tTPI1-tPGI1</i>), resulting in the plasmids T1-(<i>SmMS-SmCPS1</i>) and T1-(<i>SmMS-SmCPS1</i>)-(<i>SmMS-SmCPS1</i>). Two terminators were inserted into the scaffold plasmid, with dedicated homologous arms (L1 and L2) lying on both sides. The integration site <i>YPRCΔ15</i> was chosen as the target locus, and <i>Ura3</i> was chosen as the selection marker. Each expression cassette with designed to have homologous arms (primers: L1-F/L2-R) was amplified individually. The selection marker module and integration homologous arm module (15Site1-Ura3-L1 and L2-15site2) were also amplified. All the amplified fragments were used to co-transform BY-HZ16 for assembly and integration, and transformants were selected on synthetic dropin medium-Ura-His (SD-Ura-His) containing 20 g L<sup>−1</sup> glucose and 18 g L<sup>−1</sup> agar. Positive transformants were verified by sequencing, yielding the strains PS1 and PS2 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">23</a>). All strains are listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM1">21</a>. All primers used in vector construction are listed in Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">7</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18"><i>CYP</i> screening based on the miltiradiene-producing strain</h3><p>The gene-to-metabolite network analysis and specific-gene analysis in <i>T. wilfordii</i> were performed, and 10 highly expressed <i>CYP</i> genes were chosen as candidates for investigation. The RNAi results showed that 4 CYPs were most likely to regulate triptolide biosynthesis and were then selected to react with abietane-type diene olefin miltiradiene. First, to simplify the fermentation procedure, the constitutive promoters <i>pPGK1-pTEF2</i> were cloned into the <i>BamH</i>I and <i>Not</i>I sites of the pESC-Leu vector to replace the inducible promoters <i>pGAL1-pGAL10</i>, yielding the plasmid Leu-PT. Second, cytochrome P450 reductase 3 (CPR3) was inserted into the <i>Not</i>I site of the plasmid Leu-PT according to the pEASY-Uni Seamless Cloning and Assembly Kit (TransGen Biotech, Beijing, China), resulting in the plasmid Leu-PT-CPR3. Then, each <i>CYP</i> gene was introduced into the <i>BamH</i>I site of the plasmid Leu-PT-CPR3. All the resulting CYP expression plasmids were individually introduced into strain PS2 following the user manual of the Frozen-EZ Yeast Transformation II KitTM (Zymo Research, USA) for product identification. All primers used for <i>CYP</i> gene screening are listed in Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM5">7</a>.</p><p>Three colonies were picked for each genotype and used to inoculate 5 mL of synthetic dropin medium -Ura-His-Leu (SD-Ura-His-Leu) containing 20 g L<sup>−1</sup> glucose. The cells were grown in a shaker at 30 °C and 230 rpm for 48 h, after which the resulting seed cultures were transferred into fresh medium at a ratio of 1:50 and fermented under the same conditions for 3 days. Yeast cells were lysed using a nano homogenizer (AH-1500, ATS Engineering Limited, Canada) and then extracted twice with an equal volume of ethyl acetate. Anhydrous sodium sulfate was added to remove residual water, and the combined organic phases were dried and methylated with (trimethylsilyl)diazomethane (Aladdin Industrial Inc., Shanghai, China)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Su, P. et al. Probing the single key amino acid responsible for the novel catalytic function of ent-kaurene oxidase supported by NADPH-cytochrome P450 reductases in Tripterygium wilfordii. Front Plant Sci. 8, 1756 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2776">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Su, P. et al. Functional characterization of ent-copalyl diphosphate synthase, kaurene synthase and kaurene oxidase in the Salvia miltiorrhiza gibberellin biosynthetic pathway. Sci. Rep. 6, 23057 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2779">72</a></sup>. The methylated samples were re-dried and then dissolved in 100 μL of ethyl acetate for gas chromatography-mass spectrometry (GC-MS) using a Thermo TRACE 1310/TSQ 8000 gas chromatograph equipped with a TG-5 MS (30 m × 0.25 mm × 0.25 μm) capillary column. The GC conditions were as follows: the sample (1 μL) was injected in split mode (20:1) at 250 °C under a He flow rate of 1 mL min<sup>−1</sup>, the GC oven temperature was programmed to rise from an initial 40 °C at 20 °C min<sup>−1</sup> to 200 °C and at 15 °C min<sup>−1</sup> to 250 °C, then to 270 °C at 1.5 °C min<sup>−1</sup>. The ion trap heating temperature was 250 °C. The electron energy was 70 eV. Spectra were recorded in the range of 40–500 m/z.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">In vivo assays for TwCYP728B70 activity</h3><p>The pESC-Leu::(CPR3 + TwCYP728B70) construct was transformed into the yeast strain WAT11, which enables the catalytic activity of plant CYPs by expressing an Arabidopsis CPR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Urban, P., Mignotte, C., Kazmaier, M., Delorme, F. &amp; Pompon, D. Cloning, yeast expression, and characterization of the coupling of two distantly related Arabidopsis thaliana NADPH-cytochrome P450 reductases with P450 CYP73A5. J. Biol. Chem. 272, 19176–19186 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2800">45</a></sup>. Transformants were selected on synthetic dropin medium SD-Leu plates containing 20 g L<sup>−1</sup> glucose. The cells were grown in a shaker at 30 °C and 230 rpm for 48 h, then transferred into 50 mL of fresh medium at a ratio of 1:50 and fermented under the same conditions for 12 h. To confirm engineered yeast strains for oxidative transformation of diterpenoid substrates, miltiradiene, abietatriene, 8,12-abietadienol, or dehydroabietinol (in methanol) was added to the yeast cultures to final concentrations of 100 μM and fermented for another 48 h. Yeast cells were lysed, extracted twice with an equal volume of ethyl acetate, and methylated before GC-MS analysis, as described above.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">RNAi of the candidate <i>CYP</i> genes in <i>T. wilfordii</i>
</h3><p>The fragments of these 10 candidate <i>CYP</i> genes were amplified using Phusion® High-Fidelity DNA Polymerase (New England Biolabs, USA) and inserted into the RNAi vector pK7GWIWG2D according to the Gateway procedure (Invitrogen, USA), and the resulting vectors were verified by complete sequencing.</p><p>Suspension cells in the logarithmic growth phase were chose and precultured on Murashige and Skoog (MS) solid medium containing 0.5 mg L<sup>−1</sup> 2,4-D, 0.1 mg L<sup>−1</sup> KT, 0.5 mg L<sup>−1</sup> IBA and 30 g L<sup>−1</sup> sucrose (pH = 5.8) for 7 days. Then, the recombinant plasmid DNA mixed with Au microparticles were transformed into the suspension cells through bombardment using a biolistic gene gun (PDS 1000/He, Bio-Rad). Each transformation was carried out two times. The bombarded suspension cells were cultured for another 7 days before harvesting for qRT-PCR and UPLC analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Zhao, Y. et al. Genetic transformation system for woody plant Tripterygium wilfordii and its application to product natural celastrol. Front Plant Sci. 8, 2221 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2836">73</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Overexpression of <i>TwCYP728B70</i>
</h3><p>Vector pH7WG2D (Invitrogen) was used to overexpress <i>TwCYP728B70</i> in suspension cells following the protocol mentioned above. The resulting recombinant cells were harvested for qRT-PCR and UPLC analysis after being cultured for 3, 5, 7, and 9 days<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Zhao, Y. et al. Genetic transformation system for woody plant Tripterygium wilfordii and its application to product natural celastrol. Front Plant Sci. 8, 2221 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2855">73</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Heterologous expression of CYP in yeast and in vitro assays</h3><p>Each <i>CYP</i> gene was inserted into the <i>BamH</i>I site of the pESC-Leu vector according to the pEASY-Uni Seamless Cloning and Assembly Kit. The pESC-Leu::CYP construct was verified by complete gene sequencing, which was transformed into the yeast strain WAT11. The cells were grown first in 100 mL of SD-Leu liquid medium with 20 g L<sup>−1</sup> glucose in a shaker at 30 °C and 230 rpm to an OD<sub>600</sub> of 2–3. Cells were centrifuged and resuspended in 200 mL of yeast peptone galactose (YPL) induction medium (10 g L<sup>−1</sup> yeast extract, 20 g L<sup>−1</sup> bactopeptone, and 20 g L<sup>−1</sup> galactose) and grown for 12 h at 30 °C to induce recombinant protein expression. Microsomes were prepared based on the reported method with some modifications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Pompon, D., Louerat, B., Bronine, A. &amp; Urban, P. Yeast expression of animal and plant P450s in optimized redox environments. Methods Enzymol. 272, 51–64 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2884">74</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Guo, J. et al. CYP76AH1 catalyzes turnover of miltiradiene in tanshinones biosynthesis and enables heterologous production of ferruginol in yeasts. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 12108–12113 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d9093e2887">75</a></sup>. Briefly, the induced cells were centrifuged (1914 × <i>g</i>, 4 °C, 5 min) and resuspended in 20 mL of TEK buffer (50 mM Tris-HCl, pH 7.4, 1 mM EDTA, 0.1 M KCl), then left at room temperature for 5 min. Cells were centrifuged again (1,914 × <i>g</i>, 4 °C, 5 min) and resuspended in 50 mL of TESB buffer (50 mM Tris-HCl, pH 7.4, 1 mM EDTA, 0.6 M sorbitol), then left on ice for 10 min. The cell suspension was lysed for 7 min at 4 °C and 12,000 psi using a nano homogenize machine (AH-1500, ATS Engineering Limited, Canada), then centrifuged (17,266 × <i>g</i>, 4 °C, 15 min) to collect the supernatant. NaCl (final concentration of 0.15 M) and polyethylene glycol (PEG)−4000 (final concentration of 0.1 g mL<sup>−1</sup>) were added to the supernatant, and left on ice for 15 min. The microsomal fractions were collected by centrifugation (17,266 × <i>g</i>, 4 °C, 15 min), and resuspended in TEG buffer (50 mM Tris-HCl, pH 7.4, 1 mM EDTA, 20% (v/v) glycerol), which can be kept frozen at −80 °C for months.</p><p>In vitro enzymatic activity assays were carried out on a shaking incubator (150 rpm) at 30 °C for 4 h in 500 μL of 100 mM Tris-HCl, pH 7.5, containing 0.5 mg of total microsomal proteins and 500 μM NADPH, along with a regenerating system (consisting of 5 μM FAD, 5 μM FMN, 5 mM glucose-6-phosphate, 1 unit mL<sup>−1</sup> glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase), and 100 μM substrates. Reactions were stopped by the addition of 500 μL of methanol and used for UPLC analysis. Negative control reactions were carried out with microsomal preparations from recombinant yeast transformed with empty pESC-Leu.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Fermentation</h3><p>To engineer yeast for the production of intermediates involved in triptolide biosynthesis and obtain enough compound for structural characterization, the strain PS2 containing the plasmid Leu-PT-CPR3::TwCYP728B70 was used to inoculate 50 mL of SD-Ura-His-Leu medium in a 250 mL shake flask at 30 °C and 230 rpm for 24 h. The entire culture volume was transferred into 500 mL of fresh seed medium and incubated for 24 h, then transferred into 2 mL of fresh seed medium and incubated for another 24 h. The seed medium was then used to inoculate 8 L of fermentation medium in a New Brunswick BioFlo/CelliGen 115 bioreactor (Eppendorf, Germany) with a maximal working volume of 14 L.</p><p>The fermentation was performed at 30 °C. During fermentation, the pH was maintained at 5.0 with the automatic addition of ammonium hydroxide, the agitation rate was kept between 200 and 600 rpm, and the dissolved oxygen was kept above 40%. Concentrated glucose solution (40%, wt/vol) was fed periodically to keep the glucose concentration above 1.0 g L<sup>−1</sup>. Additionally SD-Ura-His-Leu medium was fed after the initial 30 h of fermentation. The culture was then harvested by extraction after 90 h of total fermentation time.</p><p>Yeast cells were concentrated to 1 L, then lysed using the nano homogenizer and then extracted ten times with an equal volume of ethyl acetate. Anhydrous sodium sulfate was added to remove residual water, and the organic fractions were pooled and dried using a Nitrogen Evaporator (Baojingkeji, Henan, China). The yellow oily liquid (15.5 g) was chromatographed on LiChroprep® Si60 (40–63 μm, Merck, MA, USA) with a stepwise gradient of petroleum ether-hexane (v/v, 10:1) to obtain the fraction (~500 mL). The fraction was re-dried and then dissolved in 500 μL of acetonitrile. Preparative HPLC was performed on Agilent 1260 Infinity High-Performance Liquid Chromatography System with a Shim-pack GIST C18 (250 × 4.6 mml.D., 5 μm, SHIMADZU, Kyoto, Japan). The mobile phase, consisting of a mixture of water (A) and acetonitrile (B), was pumped at a flow rate of 1 ml min<sup>−1</sup> and the eluate was monitored at 200 nm. The gradient elution was programmed as follows: 0 min at 65% B, 50 min at 65% B. The injection volume was 20 μL.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The data sets generated and analyzed during this study are available from the corresponding author upon request. The genome sequence data and transcriptome sequence data for <i>T. wilfordii</i> have been deposited under NCBI BioProject number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA542587">PRJNA542587</a> and the final assembly is available at GenBank under the accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/JAAARO000000000">JAAARO000000000</a>. The source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig2">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig3">3a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig3">3b</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-14776-1#Fig3">3f</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p>

            (Celastraceae) among five continents
                </a>
              </h3>
              
                <ul data-test="author-list"
                    class="c-article-further-reading__authors-list js-list-authors-3 js-etal-collapsed" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"
                    id="authors-3">
                  <li><span
                        class="js-separator"></span><span>Yi‐Xuan Zhu</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Feng‐Wei Lei</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Ling Tong</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Xian‐Yun Mu</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Jun Wen</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">&nbsp;&amp;&nbsp;</span><span>Zhi‐Xiang Zhang</span></li></ul>
              
              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-3">
                <i>Journal of Systematics and Evolution</i>
                (2020)
              </p>
            </li>
          
            <li class="c-article-further-reading__item js-ref-item" id="further-reading-item-4">
              <h3 class="c-article-further-reading__title">
                <a class="print-link"
                   data-track="click"
                   data-track-action="view further reading article"
                   data-track-label="link:Genes functional identification and synthetic biology of natural products"
                   href="https://doi.org/10.1016/S1875-5364(20)60002-4"
                   id="title-link-4">
                  Genes functional identification and synthetic biology of natural products
                </a>
              </h3>
              
                <ul data-test="author-list"
                    class="c-article-further-reading__authors-list js-list-authors-3 js-etal-collapsed" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"
                    id="authors-4">
                  <li><span
                        class="js-separator"></span><span>Wei GAO</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">&nbsp;&amp;&nbsp;</span><span>Lu-Qi HUANG</span></li></ul>
              
              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-4">
                <i>Chinese Journal of Natural Medicines</i>
                (2020)
              </p>
            </li>
